[PMID:29323693]\n- **Entity Name**: Lipid Metabolism Disorders\n  - **CUI**: C0154251\n  - **Semantic Type**: Disease or Syndrome\n    - An inherited metabolic disorder caused by an enzyme deficiency, resulting in an inability to oxidize fatty acids for energy production.\n    - condition in which there is a deviation or interruption in the processing of lipids in the body: synthesis, absorption, transport, storage, and utilization.\n    - <p>Metabolism is the process your body uses to make energy from the food you eat. Food is made up of proteins, carbohydrates, and fats. Chemicals in your digestive system (enzymes) break the food parts down into sugars and acids, your body's fuel. Your body can use this fuel right away, or it can store the energy in your body tissues. If you have a <a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/metabolicdisorders.html\">metabolic disorder</a>, something goes wrong with this process.</p> <p>Lipid metabolism disorders, such as <a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/gaucherdisease.html\">Gaucher disease</a> and <a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/taysachsdisease.html\">Tay-Sachs disease</a>, involve lipids. Lipids are fats or fat-like substances. They include oils, fatty acids, waxes, and cholesterol. If you have one of these disorders, you may not have enough enzymes to break down lipids. Or the enzymes may not work properly and your body can't convert the fats into energy. They cause a harmful amount of lipids to build up in your body. Over time, that can damage your cells and tissues, especially in the brain, peripheral nervous system, liver, spleen, and bone marrow. Many of these disorders can be very serious, or sometimes even fatal.</p> <p>These disorders are inherited. Newborn babies get <a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/newbornscreening.html\">screened</a> for some of them, using blood tests. If there is a family history of one of these disorders, parents can get <a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/genetictesting.html\">genetic testing</a> to see whether they carry the gene. Other genetic tests can tell whether the fetus has the disorder or carries the gene for the disorder.</p> <p>Enzyme replacement therapies can help with a few of these disorders. For others, there is no treatment. Medicines, blood transfusions, and other procedures may help with complications.</p>\n    - Pathological conditions resulting from abnormal anabolism or catabolism of lipids in the body.\n- **Entity Name**: Dyslipidemias\n  - **CUI**: C0242339\n  - **Semantic Type**: Disease or Syndrome\n    - A lipoprotein metabolism disorder characterized by decreased levels of high-density lipoproteins, or elevated levels of plasma cholesterol, low-density lipoproteins and/or triglycerides.\n    - Abnormalities in the serum levels of LIPIDS, including overproduction or deficiency.         "content": "PCOS stands for Polycystic Ovary Syndrome. It is a complex endocrine disorder affecting approximately 5% to 15% of women of reproductive age. Here are some key features:\n\n- **Characteristics**:\n  - Chronic anovulation (absence of ovulation).\n  - Hyperandrogenism (excess male hormones).\n  - Irregular menstrual cycles.\n  - Symptoms include infertility, hirsutism (excessive hair growth), and acne. \n\n- **Associated Risks**:\n  - Higher risk of type 2 diabetes and metabolic syndrome.\n  - Increased chances of heart disease and high blood pressure.\n\n- **Diagnosis**:\n  - Based on clinical evaluation, blood tests, and sometimes ultrasounds.\n\n- **Management**:\n  - No cure, but lifestyle changes (diet and exercise) and medications can help manage symptoms.\n\nFor more information, consult a healthcare provider. \n\n**Sources:**\n- [Metformin treatment before and during IVF or ICSI in women with polycystic ovary syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd006105.pub4)\n- [Chinese herbal medicine for subfertile women with polycystic ovarian syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd007535.pub4)",

Key approaches include:\n\n- **Lifestyle Changes**:\n  - Adopting a healthy diet and exercise regimen.\n  - Weight management is crucial to improve symptoms.\n\n- **Medications**:\n  - **Combined Oral Contraceptives**: First-line for regulating menstrual cycles and reducing hyperandrogenism symptoms (low-dose preferred).\n  - **Metformin**: Used to address metabolic features, especially if obesity is present.\n  - **Antiandrogens**: For hair loss or hirsutism when other treatments have been ineffective.\n  - **Letrozole**: First-line medication for infertility when ovulation induction is needed, with other medications as second-line options.\n\n- **Surgical Options**:\n  - Ovarian drilling may be considered for infertility if other treatments fail.\n\nAlways consult a healthcare professional for personalized treatment options. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [International Evidence-based Guideline for the assessment and management of polycystic ovary syndrome 2023](https://doi.org/10.26180/24003834.v1)",

Here are some key features:\n\n- **Characteristics**:\n  - Chronic anovulation (absence of ovulation).\n  - Hyperandrogenism (excess male hormones).\n  - Irregular menstrual cycles.\n  - Symptoms include infertility, hirsutism (excessive hair growth), and acne. \n\n- **Associated Risks**:\n  - Higher risk of type 2 diabetes and metabolic syndrome.\n  - Increased chances of heart disease and high blood pressure.\n\n- **Diagnosis**:\n  - Based on clinical evaluation, blood tests, and sometimes ultrasounds.\n\n- **Management**:\n  - No cure, but lifestyle changes (diet and exercise) and medications can help manage symptoms.\n\nFor more information, consult a healthcare provider. \n\n**Sources:**\n- [Metformin treatment before and during IVF or ICSI in women with polycystic ovary syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd006105.pub4)\n- [Chinese herbal medicine for subfertile women with polycystic ovarian syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd007535.pub4)",

syndrome\n- **Text**: Polycystic ovary syndrome (PCOS) is the most common female endocrinopathy, affecting up to 8% to 13% of reproductive-age women (1) and is characterized by chronic anovulation, clinical and/or biochemical hyperandrogenism (HA), and polycystic ovarian morphology that constitute the 3 diagnostic features as per the Rotterdam criteria (2) .In fact, up to 90% of the reproductive-age women with PCOS have chronic anovulation and menstrual irregularity (3) , contributing significantly to the health care-related economic burden caused by this disease (4) .Although oral contraceptives (OCPs) are recommended as first-line treatment for menstrual irregularity by most international guidelines (1, 5) , concerns still exist regarding their long-term use.Increased risks of cardiovascular diseases and thromboembolic events have been associated with the consumption of OCPs in women with multiple risk factors, including obesity, hypertension, type 2 diabetes mellitus (T2DM), and dyslipidemia (6) .Further, studies have reported weight gain (7) and worsened lipid profiles (8) in women with PCOS who consume OCPs.Therefore, the long-term risks and benefits of OCP treatment for the metabolism of women with PCOS remain controversial.Metformin, a biguanide approved for treating T2DM, is the most widely investigated and commonly applied insulin sensitizer in treating patients with PCOS who have obesity and are overweight or present metabolic abnormalities.In a recent Cochrane review (9) comparing the effects of metformin versus placebo or no treatment, a significant improvement in ovulation rate and menstrual frequency was observed in the metformin group.However, the percentage of patients showing improved menstrual cyclicity after metformin treatment was only up to 40% to 50 % (9, 10) .Half of the patients had persistent oligomenorrhea, and it is still unclear which specific subgroups of patients could restore ovulation after metformin treatment.\n- **DOI**: 10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.12.045\n- **Sequence**: 3\n### Next Section\n- **Text**: Microribonucleic acids (miRNAs) are small noncoding RNAs, 19 to 22 nucleotides, that play a critical role in posttranscriptional gene regulation (11) .They inhibit mRNA translation or induce mRNA degradation by binding to the 3 0 untranslated region of mRNA targets and participate in multiple physiologic processes including cell proliferation, differentiation, metabolism, aging, and apoptosis (12) .Dysregulated miRNA expression is involved in the pathogenesis of numerous diseases, such as cancer (13) , autoimmune diseases (14) , obesity (15) , T2DM (16) , and cardiovascular diseases (17) .Since the discovery of miRNAs in peripheral blood, there has been an increasing interest in the potential use of the circulating miRNAs as diagnostic and prognostic biomarkers of human diseases and in their use for evaluating responses to specific treatments (11) , especially because they are resistant to ribonuclease degradation and remain stable in long-frozen samples (18) .Although the involvement of miRNAs in the etiology or pathophysiology of PCOS is still poorly understood, It results in hormonal imbalances and leads to irregular and abnormal menstrual periods, excess growth of hair, acne eruptions and obesity.\n  - A complex disorder characterized by infertility, HIRSUTISM; OBESITY; and various menstrual disturbances such as OLIGOMENORRHEA; AMENORRHEA; ANOVULATION. Polycystic ovary syndrome is usually associated with bilateral enlarged ovaries studded with atretic follicles, not with cysts. The term, polycystic ovary, is misleading.\n  - clinical symptom complex characterized by presence of multiple cysts on the ovaries, oligomenorrhea or amenorrhea, anovulation and regularly associated with excessive amounts of body hair (hirsuitism), excessive body weight, infertility and insulin resistance.\n  - <p>Polycystic ovary syndrome (PCOS) happens when a woman's ovaries or adrenal glands produce more male hormones than normal. PCOS causes cysts (fluid-filled sacs) to grow on the ovaries. Symptoms include:</p><ul> <li>Irregular menstrual periods</li> <li><a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/femaleinfertility.html\">Infertility</a></li> <li><a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/pelvicpain.html\">Pelvic pain</a></li> <li>Excess hair growth on the face, chest, stomach, or thighs</li> <li>Weight gain</li> <li>Acne or oily skin</li> <li>Patches of thickened skin</li> </ul> <p>Women with PCOS are at higher risk of diabetes, metabolic syndrome, heart disease, and high blood pressure.</p> <p>PCOS is more common in women who have obesity or have a mother or sister with PCOS. To diagnose PCOS, your health care provider may do a physical exam, pelvic exam, blood tests, and an <a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/lab-tests/sonogram/\">ultrasound</a>.</p> <p>There is no cure, but diet, exercise, and medicines can help control the symptoms. Birth control pills help women have normal periods, reduce male hormone levels, and clear acne. Treatments for infertility caused by PCOS may include medicines, surgery, and <a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/assistedreproductivetechnology.html\">in vitro fertilization</a> (IVF).</p> <p class=\"\">NIH: National Institute of Child Health and Human Development</p>\n  - lifestyle remains vital throughout the lifespan in PCOS, with a strong focus on overall health, prevention of weight gain and if required, on weight management.Recognising the benefits if many specific diet and physical activity regimens, there is no one regimen that has benefits over others in PCOS.Weight bias and stigma should be minimised and healthcare professionals should seek permission to weigh women, with explanation of weight-related risks.Combined oral contraceptive pills are first-line pharmacological treatment for menstrual irregularity and hyperandrogenism, with no PCOS causes cysts (fluid-filled sacs) to grow on the ovaries. Symptoms include:</p><ul> <li>Irregular menstrual periods</li> <li><a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/femaleinfertility.html\">Infertility</a></li> <li><a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/pelvicpain.html\">Pelvic pain</a></li> <li>Excess hair growth on the face, chest, stomach, or thighs</li> <li>Weight gain</li> <li>Acne or oily skin</li> <li>Patches of thickened skin</li> </ul> <p>Women with PCOS are at higher risk of diabetes, metabolic syndrome, heart disease, and high blood pressure.</p> <p>PCOS is more common in women who have obesity or have a mother or sister with PCOS. To diagnose PCOS, your health care provider may do a physical exam, pelvic exam, blood tests, and an <a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/lab-tests/sonogram/\">ultrasound</a>.</p> <p>There is no cure, but diet, exercise, and medicines can help control the symptoms. Birth control pills help women have normal periods, reduce male hormone levels, and clear acne. Treatments for infertility caused by PCOS may include medicines, surgery, and <a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/assistedreproductivetechnology.html\">in vitro fertilization</a> (IVF).</p> <p vessels, and appearance\n\n### Section Details\n- **Title**: The role of circulating miRNAs in mechanism of action and prediction of therapeutic responses of metformin in polycystic ovarian syndrome\n- **Text**: Polycystic ovary syndrome (PCOS) is the most common female endocrinopathy, affecting up to 8% to 13% of reproductive-age women (1) and is characterized by chronic anovulation, clinical and/or biochemical hyperandrogenism (HA), and polycystic ovarian morphology that constitute the 3 diagnostic features as per the Rotterdam criteria (2) .In fact, up to 90% of the reproductive-age women with PCOS have chronic anovulation and menstrual irregularity (3) , contributing significantly to the health care-related economic burden caused by this disease (4) .Although oral contraceptives (OCPs) are recommended as first-line treatment for menstrual irregularity by most international guidelines (1, 5) , concerns still exist regarding their long-term use.Increased risks of cardiovascular diseases and thromboembolic events have been associated with the consumption of OCPs in women with multiple risk factors, including obesity, hypertension, type 2 diabetes mellitus (T2DM), and dyslipidemia (6) .Further, studies have reported weight gain (7) and worsened lipid profiles (8) in women with PCOS who consume OCPs.Therefore, the long-term risks and benefits of OCP treatment for the metabolism of women with PCOS remain controversial.Metformin, a biguanide approved for treating T2DM, is the most widely investigated and commonly applied insulin sensitizer in treating patients with PCOS who have obesity and are overweight or present metabolic abnormalities.In a recent Cochrane review (9) comparing the effects of metformin versus placebo or no treatment, a significant improvement in ovulation rate and menstrual frequency was observed in the metformin group.However, the percentage of patients showing improved menstrual cyclicity after metformin treatment was only up to 40% to 50 % (9, 10) .Half of the patients had persistent oligomenorrhea, and it is still unclear which specific subgroups of patients could restore ovulation after metformin treatment.\n- **DOI**: 10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.12.045\n- **Sequence**: 3\n### Next Section\n- **Text**: Microribonucleic acids (miRNAs) are small noncoding RNAs, 19 to 22 nucleotides, that play a critical role in posttranscriptional gene regulation (11) .They inhibit mRNA translation or induce mRNA degradation by binding to the 3 0 untranslated region of mRNA targets and participate in multiple physiologic processes including cell proliferation, differentiation, metabolism, aging, and apoptosis (12) .Dysregulated miRNA expression is involved in the pathogenesis of numerous diseases, such as cancer (13) , autoimmune diseases (14) , obesity (15) , T2DM (16) , and cardiovascular diseases (17) .Since the discovery of miRNAs in peripheral blood, there has been an increasing interest in the potential use of the circulating miRNAs as diagnostic and prognostic biomarkers of human diseases and in their use for evaluating responses to specific treatments (11) , especially because they are resistant to ribonuclease degradation and remain stable in long-frozen samples (18) .Although the involvement of miRNAs in the etiology or pathophysiology of PCOS is still poorly understood, several studies have demonstrated altered expression of certain serum miRNAs in PCOS (19) (20) (21) (22) , and hence, the application of circulating miRNAs as diagnostic biomarkers is considered promising (19, 23) .\n- **DOI**: 10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.12.045\n- **Sequence**: 4\n### Entities in Section\n- **Entity Name**: Oligomenorrhea\n  - **CUI**: C0028949\n  - **Semantic Type**: Consider also CLIENTS.\n- **Entity Name**: Obesity\n  - **CUI**: C0028754\n  - **Semantic Type**: Disease or Syndrome\n    - A status with BODY WEIGHT that is grossly above the recommended standards, usually due to accumulation of excess FATS in the body. The standards may vary with age, sex, genetic or cultural background. In the BODY MASS INDEX, a BMI greater than 30.0 kg/m2 is considered obese, and a BMI greater than 40.0 kg/m2 is considered morbidly obese (MORBID OBESITY).\n    - Having a high amount of body fat (body mass index [BMI] of 30 or more).\n    - excessively high accumulation of body fat or adipose tissue in relation to lean body mass; the amount of body fat (or adiposity) includes concern for both the distribution of fat throughout the body and the size of the adipose tissue deposits; individuals are usually at high clinical risk because of excess amount of body fat (BMI greater than 30).\n    - <p>Obesity means having too much body fat. It is different from being overweight, which means weighing too much. The weight may come from muscle, bone, fat, and/or body water. Both terms mean that a person's weight is greater than what's considered healthy for his or her height. </p> <p>Obesity happens over time when you eat more calories than you use. The balance between calories-in and calories-out differs for each person. Factors that might affect your weight include your genetic makeup, overeating, eating high-fat foods, and not being physically active. </p> <p>Obesity increases your risk of diabetes, heart disease, stroke, arthritis, and some cancers. If you have obesity, losing even 5 to 10% of your weight can delay or prevent some of these diseases. For example, that means losing 10 to 20 pounds if you weigh 200 pounds.</p> <p class=\"\">NIH: National Institute of Diabetes and Digestive and Kidney Diseases</p>\n    - A condition in which an individual accumulates excessive fat for age and gender that exceeds overweight.\n    - Accumulation of substantial excess body fat.         "content": "Treatment for Polycystic Ovary Syndrome (PCOS) focuses on managing symptoms and reducing associated risks. Key approaches include:\n\n- **Lifestyle Changes**:\n  - Adopting a healthy diet and exercise regimen.\n  - Weight management is crucial to improve symptoms.\n\n- **Medications**:\n  - **Combined Oral Contraceptives**: First-line for regulating menstrual cycles and reducing hyperandrogenism symptoms (low-dose preferred).\n  - **Metformin**: Used to address metabolic features, especially if obesity is present.\n  - **Antiandrogens**: For hair loss or hirsutism when other treatments have been ineffective.\n  - **Letrozole**: First-line medication for infertility when ovulation induction is needed, with other medications as second-line options.\n\n- **Surgical Options**:\n  - Ovarian drilling may be considered for infertility if other treatments fail.\n\nAlways consult a healthcare professional for personalized treatment options. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [International Evidence-based Guideline for the assessment and management of polycystic ovary syndrome 2023](https://doi.org/10.26180/24003834.v1)",

            "content": "IVF stands for In Vitro Fertilization, a complex assisted reproductive technology used to help individuals or couples conceive. Here are the key steps involved:\n\n- **Ovarian Stimulation**:\n  - Hormonal medications are administered to stimulate the ovaries to produce multiple eggs.\n\n- **Egg Retrieval**:\n  - Once the eggs are mature, a minor surgical procedure is performed to retrieve them from the ovaries.\n\n- **Fertilization**:\n  - The retrieved eggs are combined with sperm in a laboratory setting. This can be done via standard insemination or intracytoplasmic sperm injection (ICSI).\n\n- **Embryo Culture**:\n  - Fertilized eggs (embryos) are cultured for several days to monitor development.\n\n- **Embryo Transfer**:\n  - One or more healthy embryos are transferred into the uterus.\n\n- **Pregnancy Test**:\n  - A pregnancy test is conducted a couple of weeks after the transfer to check for success.\n\nIVF is often recommended for individuals facing infertility issues, such as blocked fallopian tubes, endometriosis, or male factor infertility. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [Reproductive Medicine and Biology, 2023](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12473) \n- [Assisted reproductive technology surveillance \u2014 United States, 2019](https://doi.org/10.15585/mmwr.mm6935a2)",

                "content": "PCOS stands for Polycystic Ovary Syndrome. It is a complex endocrine disorder affecting approximately 5% to 15% of women of reproductive age. Here are some key features:\n\n- **Characteristics**:\n  - Chronic anovulation (absence of ovulation).\n  - Hyperandrogenism (excess male hormones).\n  - Irregular menstrual cycles.\n  - Symptoms include infertility, hirsutism (excessive hair growth), and acne. \n\n- **Associated Risks**:\n  - Higher risk of type 2 diabetes and metabolic syndrome.\n  - Increased chances of heart disease and high blood pressure.\n\n- **Diagnosis**:\n  - Based on clinical evaluation, blood tests, and sometimes ultrasounds.\n\n- **Management**:\n  - No cure, but lifestyle changes (diet and exercise) and medications can help manage symptoms.\n\nFor more information, consult a healthcare provider. \n\n**Sources:**\n- [Metformin treatment before and during IVF or ICSI in women with polycystic ovary syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd006105.pub4)\n- [Chinese herbal medicine for subfertile women with polycystic ovarian syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd007535.pub4)",

        "content": "IVF stands for In Vitro Fertilization, a complex assisted reproductive technology used to help individuals or couples conceive. Here are the key steps involved:\n\n- **Ovarian Stimulation**:\n  - Hormonal medications are administered to stimulate the ovaries to produce multiple eggs.\n\n- **Egg Retrieval**:\n  - Once the eggs are mature, a minor surgical procedure is performed to retrieve them from the ovaries.\n\n- **Fertilization**:\n  - The retrieved eggs are combined with sperm in a laboratory setting. This can be done via standard insemination or intracytoplasmic sperm injection (ICSI).\n\n- **Embryo Culture**:\n  - Fertilized eggs (embryos) are cultured for several days to monitor development.\n\n- **Embryo Transfer**:\n  - One or more healthy embryos are transferred into the uterus.\n\n- **Pregnancy Test**:\n  - A pregnancy test is conducted a couple of weeks after the transfer to check for success.\n\nIVF is often recommended for individuals facing infertility issues, such as blocked fallopian tubes, endometriosis, or male factor infertility. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [Reproductive Medicine and Biology, 2023](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12473) \n- [Assisted reproductive technology surveillance \u2014 United States, 2019](https://doi.org/10.15585/mmwr.mm6935a2)",

It\u2019s important to discuss potential risks with a healthcare provider and have a supportive plan in place.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Fertility and Sterility, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008) \n- [International Society for Mild Approaches in Assisted Reproduction (ISMAR)](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12480)",

            {
                "content": "PCOS stands for Polycystic Ovary Syndrome. It is a complex endocrine disorder affecting approximately 5% to 15% of women of reproductive age. Here are some key features:\n\n- **Characteristics**:\n  - Chronic anovulation (absence of ovulation).\n  - Hyperandrogenism (excess male hormones).\n  - Irregular menstrual cycles.\n  - Symptoms include infertility, hirsutism (excessive hair growth), and acne. \n\n- **Associated Risks**:\n  - Higher risk of type 2 diabetes and metabolic syndrome.\n  - Increased chances of heart disease and high blood pressure.\n\n- **Diagnosis**:\n  - Based on clinical evaluation, blood tests, and sometimes ultrasounds.\n\n- **Management**:\n  - No cure, but lifestyle changes (diet and exercise) and medications can help manage symptoms.\n\nFor more information, consult a healthcare provider. \n\n**Sources:**\n- [Metformin treatment before and during IVF or ICSI in women with polycystic ovary syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd006105.pub4)\n- [Chinese herbal medicine for subfertile women with polycystic ovarian syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd007535.pub4)",
                "role": "assistant"

                "content": "and how can this be treated ?",
                "role": "user"
            },
            {
                "content": "Treatment for Polycystic Ovary Syndrome (PCOS) focuses on managing symptoms and reducing associated risks. Key approaches include:\n\n- **Lifestyle Changes**:\n  - Adopting a healthy diet and exercise regimen.\n  - Weight management is crucial to improve symptoms.\n\n- **Medications**:\n  - **Combined Oral Contraceptives**: First-line for regulating menstrual cycles and reducing hyperandrogenism symptoms (low-dose preferred).\n  - **Metformin**: Used to address metabolic features, especially if obesity is present.\n  - **Antiandrogens**: For hair loss or hirsutism when other treatments have been ineffective.\n  - **Letrozole**: First-line medication for infertility when ovulation induction is needed, with other medications as second-line options.\n\n- **Surgical Options**:\n  - Ovarian drilling may be considered for infertility if other treatments fail.\n\nAlways consult a healthcare professional for personalized treatment options. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [International Evidence-based Guideline for the assessment and management of polycystic ovary syndrome 2023](https://doi.org/10.26180/24003834.v1)",

                "content": "IVF stands for In Vitro Fertilization, a complex assisted reproductive technology used to help individuals or couples conceive. Here are the key steps involved:\n\n- **Ovarian Stimulation**:\n  - Hormonal medications are administered to stimulate the ovaries to produce multiple eggs.\n\n- **Egg Retrieval**:\n  - Once the eggs are mature, a minor surgical procedure is performed to retrieve them from the ovaries.\n\n- **Fertilization**:\n  - The retrieved eggs are combined with sperm in a laboratory setting. This can be done via standard insemination or intracytoplasmic sperm injection (ICSI).\n\n- **Embryo Culture**:\n  - Fertilized eggs (embryos) are cultured for several days to monitor development.\n\n- **Embryo Transfer**:\n  - One or more healthy embryos are transferred into the uterus.\n\n- **Pregnancy Test**:\n  - A pregnancy test is conducted a couple of weeks after the transfer to check for success.\n\nIVF is often recommended for individuals facing infertility issues, such as blocked fallopian tubes, endometriosis, or male factor infertility. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [Reproductive Medicine and Biology, 2023](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12473) \n- [Assisted reproductive technology surveillance \u2014 United States, 2019](https://doi.org/10.15585/mmwr.mm6935a2)",

        "content": "IVF (In Vitro Fertilization) is generally considered safe, but there are potential risks and complications that individuals should be aware of:\n\n- **Ovarian Hyperstimulation Syndrome (OHSS)**:\n  - Can occur due to stimulation medications, leading to painful swelling of the ovaries.\n\n- **Multiple Pregnancies**:\n  - Higher chance of twins or multiples, which can increase risks for both mother and babies.\n\n- **Ectopic Pregnancy**:\n  - A rare occurrence where an embryo implants outside the uterus, typically in a fallopian tube.\n\n- **Surgical Risks**:\n  - Risks associated with egg retrieval procedures, including bleeding, infection, and injury to surrounding organs.\n\n- **Emotional and Psychological Impact**:\n  - The IVF process can be emotionally taxing and lead to stress or anxiety for individuals and couples.\n\nWhile these risks exist, many individuals undergo IVF successfully and safely. It\u2019s important to discuss potential risks with a healthcare provider and have a supportive plan in place.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Fertility and Sterility, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008) \n- [International Society for Mild Approaches in Assisted Reproduction (ISMAR)](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12480)",

Here are some shared aspects:\n\n- **Age**:\n  - Advanced age affects fertility in both genders, with declining fertility rates as individuals get older.\n\n- **Hormonal Imbalances**:\n  - Hormonal issues can impact ovulation in women and sperm production in men.\n\n- **Lifestyle Factors**:\n  - Smoking, excessive alcohol consumption, poor diet, and lack of exercise can negatively affect fertility for both sexes.\n\n- **Medical Conditions**:\n  - Conditions like diabetes, thyroid disorders, and autoimmune diseases can influence reproductive health in both men and women.\n\n- **Obesity and Underweight**:\n  - Both extreme weight conditions can disrupt hormonal balance and affect fertility.\n\n- **Stress**:\n  - Psychological stress can impact both partners, potentially leading to fertility problems.\n\nWhile there are unique causes of infertility for each gender, addressing these common factors can improve overall reproductive health for both men and women.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Fertility and Sterility, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008) \n- [Human Reproduction, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1093/humrep/deac023)",

                "content": "PCOS stands for Polycystic Ovary Syndrome. It is a complex endocrine disorder affecting approximately 5% to 15% of women of reproductive age. Here are some key features:\n\n- **Characteristics**:\n  - Chronic anovulation (absence of ovulation).\n  - Hyperandrogenism (excess male hormones).\n  - Irregular menstrual cycles.\n  - Symptoms include infertility, hirsutism (excessive hair growth), and acne. \n\n- **Associated Risks**:\n  - Higher risk of type 2 diabetes and metabolic syndrome.\n  - Increased chances of heart disease and high blood pressure.\n\n- **Diagnosis**:\n  - Based on clinical evaluation, blood tests, and sometimes ultrasounds.\n\n- **Management**:\n  - No cure, but lifestyle changes (diet and exercise) and medications can help manage symptoms.\n\nFor more information, consult a healthcare provider. \n\n**Sources:**\n- [Metformin treatment before and during IVF or ICSI in women with polycystic ovary syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd006105.pub4)\n- [Chinese herbal medicine for subfertile women with polycystic ovarian syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd007535.pub4)",

                "role": "user"

            {
                "content": "Treatment for Polycystic Ovary Syndrome (PCOS) focuses on managing symptoms and reducing associated risks. Key approaches include:\n\n- **Lifestyle Changes**:\n  - Adopting a healthy diet and exercise regimen.\n  - Weight management is crucial to improve symptoms.\n\n- **Medications**:\n  - **Combined Oral Contraceptives**: First-line for regulating menstrual cycles and reducing hyperandrogenism symptoms (low-dose preferred).\n  - **Metformin**: Used to address metabolic features, especially if obesity is present.\n  - **Antiandrogens**: For hair loss or hirsutism when other treatments have been ineffective.\n  - **Letrozole**: First-line medication for infertility when ovulation induction is needed, with other medications as second-line options.\n\n- **Surgical Options**:\n  - Ovarian drilling may be considered for infertility if other treatments fail.\n\nAlways consult a healthcare professional for personalized treatment options. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [International Evidence-based Guideline for the assessment and management of polycystic ovary syndrome 2023](https://doi.org/10.26180/24003834.v1)",

                "content": "what is ivf",
                "role": "user"
            },
            {
                "content": "IVF stands for In Vitro Fertilization, a complex assisted reproductive technology used to help individuals or couples conceive. Here are the key steps involved:\n\n- **Ovarian Stimulation**:\n  - Hormonal medications are administered to stimulate the ovaries to produce multiple eggs.\n\n- **Egg Retrieval**:\n  - Once the eggs are mature, a minor surgical procedure is performed to retrieve them from the ovaries.\n\n- **Fertilization**:\n  - The retrieved eggs are combined with sperm in a laboratory setting. This can be done via standard insemination or intracytoplasmic sperm injection (ICSI).\n\n- **Embryo Culture**:\n  - Fertilized eggs (embryos) are cultured for several days to monitor development.\n\n- **Embryo Transfer**:\n  - One or more healthy embryos are transferred into the uterus.\n\n- **Pregnancy Test**:\n  - A pregnancy test is conducted a couple of weeks after the transfer to check for success.\n\nIVF is often recommended for individuals facing infertility issues, such as blocked fallopian tubes, endometriosis, or male factor infertility. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [Reproductive Medicine and Biology, 2023](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12473) \n- [Assisted reproductive technology surveillance \u2014 United States, 2019](https://doi.org/10.15585/mmwr.mm6935a2)",
                "role": "assistant"
            },

                "content": "IVF (In Vitro Fertilization) is generally considered safe, but there are potential risks and complications that individuals should be aware of:\n\n- **Ovarian Hyperstimulation Syndrome (OHSS)**:\n  - Can occur due to stimulation medications, leading to painful swelling of the ovaries.\n\n- **Multiple Pregnancies**:\n  - Higher chance of twins or multiples, which can increase risks for both mother and babies.\n\n- **Ectopic Pregnancy**:\n  - A rare occurrence where an embryo implants outside the uterus, typically in a fallopian tube.\n\n- **Surgical Risks**:\n  - Risks associated with egg retrieval procedures, including bleeding, infection, and injury to surrounding organs.\n\n- **Emotional and Psychological Impact**:\n  - The IVF process can be emotionally taxing and lead to stress or anxiety for individuals and couples.\n\nWhile these risks exist, many individuals undergo IVF successfully and safely. It\u2019s important to discuss potential risks with a healthcare provider and have a supportive plan in place.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Fertility and Sterility, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008) \n- [International Society for Mild Approaches in Assisted Reproduction (ISMAR)](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12480)",

Here are some shared aspects:\n\n- **Age**:\n  - Advanced age affects fertility in both genders, with declining fertility rates as individuals get older.\n\n- **Hormonal Imbalances**:\n  - Hormonal issues can impact ovulation in women and sperm production in men.\n\n- **Lifestyle Factors**:\n  - Smoking, excessive alcohol consumption, poor diet, and lack of exercise can negatively affect fertility for both sexes.\n\n- **Medical Conditions**:\n  - Conditions like diabetes, thyroid disorders, and autoimmune diseases can influence reproductive health in both men and women.\n\n- **Obesity and Underweight**:\n  - Both extreme weight conditions can disrupt hormonal balance and affect fertility.\n\n- **Stress**:\n  - Psychological stress can impact both partners, potentially leading to fertility problems.\n\nWhile there are unique causes of infertility for each gender, addressing these common factors can improve overall reproductive health for both men and women.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Fertility and Sterility, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008) \n- [Human Reproduction, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1093/humrep/deac023)",

Here are some key causes and factors:\n\n- **Sperm Production Issues**:\n  - Low sperm count (oligospermia) or no sperm (azoospermia) can arise from genetic defects, hormonal imbalances, or infections.\n\n- **Sperm Function Problems**:\n  - Abnormal sperm shape (morphology) or reduced motility (movement) can hinder sperm's ability to fertilize an egg.\n\n- **Anatomical Issues**:\n  - Conditions such as varicocele (enlarged veins in the scrotum) or obstruction in the reproductive tract can block sperm passage.\n\n- **Lifestyle Factors**:\n  - Smoking, excessive alcohol use, drug abuse, obesity, and poor diet negatively impact sperm health.\n\n- **Environmental Factors**:\n  - Exposure to certain chemicals, radiation, and elevated temperatures can affect sperm production.\n\n- **Medical Conditions**:\n  - Conditions like diabetes, thyroid disorders, and sexually transmitted infections can contribute to infertility.\n\nDiagnosis typically involves semen analysis and hormone testing.                 "content": "PCOS stands for Polycystic Ovary Syndrome. It is a complex endocrine disorder affecting approximately 5% to 15% of women of reproductive age. Here are some key features:\n\n- **Characteristics**:\n  - Chronic anovulation (absence of ovulation).\n  - Hyperandrogenism (excess male hormones).\n  - Irregular menstrual cycles.\n  - Symptoms include infertility, hirsutism (excessive hair growth), and acne. \n\n- **Associated Risks**:\n  - Higher risk of type 2 diabetes and metabolic syndrome.\n  - Increased chances of heart disease and high blood pressure.\n\n- **Diagnosis**:\n  - Based on clinical evaluation, blood tests, and sometimes ultrasounds.\n\n- **Management**:\n  - No cure, but lifestyle changes (diet and exercise) and medications can help manage symptoms.\n\nFor more information, consult a healthcare provider. \n\n**Sources:**\n- [Metformin treatment before and during IVF or ICSI in women with polycystic ovary syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd006105.pub4)\n- [Chinese herbal medicine for subfertile women with polycystic ovarian syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd007535.pub4)",

                "role": "user"

            {
                "content": "Treatment for Polycystic Ovary Syndrome (PCOS) focuses on managing symptoms and reducing associated risks. Key approaches include:\n\n- **Lifestyle Changes**:\n  - Adopting a healthy diet and exercise regimen.\n  - Weight management is crucial to improve symptoms.\n\n- **Medications**:\n  - **Combined Oral Contraceptives**: First-line for regulating menstrual cycles and reducing hyperandrogenism symptoms (low-dose preferred).\n  - **Metformin**: Used to address metabolic features, especially if obesity is present.\n  - **Antiandrogens**: For hair loss or hirsutism when other treatments have been ineffective.\n  - **Letrozole**: First-line medication for infertility when ovulation induction is needed, with other medications as second-line options.\n\n- **Surgical Options**:\n  - Ovarian drilling may be considered for infertility if other treatments fail.\n\nAlways consult a healthcare professional for personalized treatment options. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [International Evidence-based Guideline for the assessment and management of polycystic ovary syndrome 2023](https://doi.org/10.26180/24003834.v1)",

                "content": "what is ivf",
                "role": "user"
            },
            {
                "content": "IVF stands for In Vitro Fertilization, a complex assisted reproductive technology used to help individuals or couples conceive. Here are the key steps involved:\n\n- **Ovarian Stimulation**:\n  - Hormonal medications are administered to stimulate the ovaries to produce multiple eggs.\n\n- **Egg Retrieval**:\n  - Once the eggs are mature, a minor surgical procedure is performed to retrieve them from the ovaries.\n\n- **Fertilization**:\n  - The retrieved eggs are combined with sperm in a laboratory setting. This can be done via standard insemination or intracytoplasmic sperm injection (ICSI).\n\n- **Embryo Culture**:\n  - Fertilized eggs (embryos) are cultured for several days to monitor development.\n\n- **Embryo Transfer**:\n  - One or more healthy embryos are transferred into the uterus.\n\n- **Pregnancy Test**:\n  - A pregnancy test is conducted a couple of weeks after the transfer to check for success.\n\nIVF is often recommended for individuals facing infertility issues, such as blocked fallopian tubes, endometriosis, or male factor infertility. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [Reproductive Medicine and Biology, 2023](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12473) \n- [Assisted reproductive technology surveillance \u2014 United States, 2019](https://doi.org/10.15585/mmwr.mm6935a2)",
                "role": "assistant"
            },

Here are some key features:\n\n- **Characteristics**:\n  - Chronic anovulation (absence of ovulation).\n  - Hyperandrogenism (excess male hormones).\n  - Irregular menstrual cycles.\n  - Symptoms include infertility, hirsutism (excessive hair growth), and acne. \n\n- **Associated Risks**:\n  - Higher risk of type 2 diabetes and metabolic syndrome.\n  - Increased chances of heart disease and high blood pressure.\n\n- **Diagnosis**:\n  - Based on clinical evaluation, blood tests, and sometimes ultrasounds.\n\n- **Management**:\n  - No cure, but lifestyle changes (diet and exercise) and medications can help manage symptoms.\n\nFor more information, consult a healthcare provider. \n\n**Sources:**\n- [Metformin treatment before and during IVF or ICSI in women with polycystic ovary syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd006105.pub4)\n- [Chinese herbal medicine for subfertile women with polycystic ovarian syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd007535.pub4)",
                "role": "assistant"

                "content": "and how can this be treated ?",
                "role": "user"
            },
            {
                "content": "Treatment for Polycystic Ovary Syndrome (PCOS) focuses on managing symptoms and reducing associated risks. Key approaches include:\n\n- **Lifestyle Changes**:\n  - Adopting a healthy diet and exercise regimen.\n  - Weight management is crucial to improve symptoms.\n\n- **Medications**:\n  - **Combined Oral Contraceptives**: First-line for regulating menstrual cycles and reducing hyperandrogenism symptoms (low-dose preferred).\n  - **Metformin**: Used to address metabolic features, especially if obesity is present.\n  - **Antiandrogens**: For hair loss or hirsutism when other treatments have been ineffective.\n  - **Letrozole**: First-line medication for infertility when ovulation induction is needed, with other medications as second-line options.\n\n- **Surgical Options**:\n  - Ovarian drilling may be considered for infertility if other treatments fail.\n\nAlways consult a healthcare professional for personalized treatment options. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [International Evidence-based Guideline for the assessment and management of polycystic ovary syndrome 2023](https://doi.org/10.26180/24003834.v1)",
                "role": "assistant"

            {
                "content": "what is ivf",
                "role": "user"
            },
            {
                "content": "IVF stands for In Vitro Fertilization, a complex assisted reproductive technology used to help individuals or couples conceive. Here are the key steps involved:\n\n- **Ovarian Stimulation**:\n  - Hormonal medications are administered to stimulate the ovaries to produce multiple eggs.\n\n- **Egg Retrieval**:\n  - Once the eggs are mature, a minor surgical procedure is performed to retrieve them from the ovaries.\n\n- **Fertilization**:\n  - The retrieved eggs are combined with sperm in a laboratory setting. This can be done via standard insemination or intracytoplasmic sperm injection (ICSI).\n\n- **Embryo Culture**:\n  - Fertilized eggs (embryos) are cultured for several days to monitor development.\n\n- **Embryo Transfer**:\n  - One or more healthy embryos are transferred into the uterus.\n\n- **Pregnancy Test**:\n  - A pregnancy test is conducted a couple of weeks after the transfer to check for success.\n\nIVF is often recommended for individuals facing infertility issues, such as blocked fallopian tubes, endometriosis, or male factor infertility. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [Reproductive Medicine and Biology, 2023](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12473) \n- [Assisted reproductive technology surveillance \u2014 United States, 2019](https://doi.org/10.15585/mmwr.mm6935a2)",
                "role": "assistant"
            },
            {
                "content": "is it dangerous?",
                "role": "user"
            },
            {
                "content": "IVF (In Vitro Fertilization) is generally considered safe, but there are potential risks and complications that individuals should be aware of:\n\n- **Ovarian Hyperstimulation Syndrome (OHSS)**:\n  - Can occur due to stimulation medications, leading to painful swelling of the ovaries.\n\n- **Multiple Pregnancies**:\n  - Higher chance of twins or multiples, which can increase risks for both mother and babies.\n\n- **Ectopic Pregnancy**:\n  - A rare occurrence where an embryo implants outside the uterus, typically in a fallopian tube.\n\n- **Surgical Risks**:\n  - Risks associated with egg retrieval procedures, including bleeding, infection, and injury to surrounding organs.\n\n- **Emotional and Psychological Impact**:\n  - The IVF process can be emotionally taxing and lead to stress or anxiety for individuals and couples.\n\nWhile these risks exist, many individuals undergo IVF successfully and safely. It\u2019s important to discuss potential risks with a healthcare provider and have a supportive plan in place.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Fertility and Sterility, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008) \n- [International Society for Mild Approaches in Assisted Reproduction (ISMAR)](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12480)",
                "role": "assistant"

            {
                "content": "Yes, there are common factors that can contribute to infertility in both males and females. Here are some shared aspects:\n\n- **Age**:\n  - Advanced age affects fertility in both genders, with declining fertility rates as individuals get older.\n\n- **Hormonal Imbalances**:\n  - Hormonal issues can impact ovulation in women and sperm production in men.\n\n- **Lifestyle Factors**:\n  - Smoking, excessive alcohol consumption, poor diet, and lack of exercise can negatively affect fertility for both sexes.\n\n- **Medical Conditions**:\n  - Conditions like diabetes, thyroid disorders, and autoimmune diseases can influence reproductive health in both men and women.\n\n- **Obesity and Underweight**:\n  - Both extreme weight conditions can disrupt hormonal balance and affect fertility.\n\n- **Stress**:\n  - Psychological stress can impact both partners, potentially leading to fertility problems.\n\nWhile there are unique causes of infertility for each gender, addressing these common factors can improve overall reproductive health for both men and women.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Fertility and Sterility, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008) \n- [Human Reproduction, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1093/humrep/deac023)",

Here are some key causes and factors:\n\n- **Sperm Production Issues**:\n  - Low sperm count (oligospermia) or no sperm (azoospermia) can arise from genetic defects, hormonal imbalances, or infections.\n\n- **Sperm Function Problems**:\n  - Abnormal sperm shape (morphology) or reduced motility (movement) can hinder sperm's ability to fertilize an egg.\n\n- **Anatomical Issues**:\n  - Conditions such as varicocele (enlarged veins in the scrotum) or obstruction in the reproductive tract can block sperm passage.\n\n- **Lifestyle Factors**:\n  - Smoking, excessive alcohol use, drug abuse, obesity, and poor diet negatively impact sperm health.\n\n- **Environmental Factors**:\n  - Exposure to certain chemicals, radiation, and elevated temperatures can affect sperm production.\n\n- **Medical Conditions**:\n  - Conditions like diabetes, thyroid disorders, and sexually transmitted infections can contribute to infertility.\n\nDiagnosis typically involves semen analysis and hormone testing.                 "content": "PCOS stands for Polycystic Ovary Syndrome. It is a complex endocrine disorder affecting approximately 5% to 15% of women of reproductive age. Here are some key features:\n\n- **Characteristics**:\n  - Chronic anovulation (absence of ovulation).\n  - Hyperandrogenism (excess male hormones).\n  - Irregular menstrual cycles.\n  - Symptoms include infertility, hirsutism (excessive hair growth), and acne. \n\n- **Associated Risks**:\n  - Higher risk of type 2 diabetes and metabolic syndrome.\n  - Increased chances of heart disease and high blood pressure.\n\n- **Diagnosis**:\n  - Based on clinical evaluation, blood tests, and sometimes ultrasounds.\n\n- **Management**:\n  - No cure, but lifestyle changes (diet and exercise) and medications can help manage symptoms.\n\nFor more information, consult a healthcare provider. \n\n**Sources:**\n- [Metformin treatment before and during IVF or ICSI in women with polycystic ovary syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd006105.pub4)\n- [Chinese herbal medicine for subfertile women with polycystic ovarian syndrome](https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.cd007535.pub4)",

            {
                "content": "Treatment for Polycystic Ovary Syndrome (PCOS) focuses on managing symptoms and reducing associated risks. Key approaches include:\n\n- **Lifestyle Changes**:\n  - Adopting a healthy diet and exercise regimen.\n  - Weight management is crucial to improve symptoms.\n\n- **Medications**:\n  - **Combined Oral Contraceptives**: First-line for regulating menstrual cycles and reducing hyperandrogenism symptoms (low-dose preferred).\n  - **Metformin**: Used to address metabolic features, especially if obesity is present.\n  - **Antiandrogens**: For hair loss or hirsutism when other treatments have been ineffective.\n  - **Letrozole**: First-line medication for infertility when ovulation induction is needed, with other medications as second-line options.\n\n- **Surgical Options**:\n  - Ovarian drilling may be considered for infertility if other treatments fail.\n\nAlways consult a healthcare professional for personalized treatment options. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [International Evidence-based Guideline for the assessment and management of polycystic ovary syndrome 2023](https://doi.org/10.26180/24003834.v1)",

                "content": "what is ivf",
                "role": "user"
            },
            {
                "content": "IVF stands for In Vitro Fertilization, a complex assisted reproductive technology used to help individuals or couples conceive. Here are the key steps involved:\n\n- **Ovarian Stimulation**:\n  - Hormonal medications are administered to stimulate the ovaries to produce multiple eggs.\n\n- **Egg Retrieval**:\n  - Once the eggs are mature, a minor surgical procedure is performed to retrieve them from the ovaries.\n\n- **Fertilization**:\n  - The retrieved eggs are combined with sperm in a laboratory setting. This can be done via standard insemination or intracytoplasmic sperm injection (ICSI).\n\n- **Embryo Culture**:\n  - Fertilized eggs (embryos) are cultured for several days to monitor development.\n\n- **Embryo Transfer**:\n  - One or more healthy embryos are transferred into the uterus.\n\n- **Pregnancy Test**:\n  - A pregnancy test is conducted a couple of weeks after the transfer to check for success.\n\nIVF is often recommended for individuals facing infertility issues, such as blocked fallopian tubes, endometriosis, or male factor infertility. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [Reproductive Medicine and Biology, 2023](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12473) \n- [Assisted reproductive technology surveillance \u2014 United States, 2019](https://doi.org/10.15585/mmwr.mm6935a2)",
                "role": "assistant"
            },

                "content": "is it dangerous?",
                "role": "user"
            },
            {
                "content": "IVF (In Vitro Fertilization) is generally considered safe, but there are potential risks and complications that individuals should be aware of:\n\n- **Ovarian Hyperstimulation Syndrome (OHSS)**:\n  - Can occur due to stimulation medications, leading to painful swelling of the ovaries.\n\n- **Multiple Pregnancies**:\n  - Higher chance of twins or multiples, which can increase risks for both mother and babies.\n\n- **Ectopic Pregnancy**:\n  - A rare occurrence where an embryo implants outside the uterus, typically in a fallopian tube.\n\n- **Surgical Risks**:\n  - Risks associated with egg retrieval procedures, including bleeding, infection, and injury to surrounding organs.\n\n- **Emotional and Psychological Impact**:\n  - The IVF process can be emotionally taxing and lead to stress or anxiety for individuals and couples.\n\nWhile these risks exist, many individuals undergo IVF successfully and safely. It\u2019s important to discuss potential risks with a healthcare provider and have a supportive plan in place.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Fertility and Sterility, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008) \n- [International Society for Mild Approaches in Assisted Reproduction (ISMAR)](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12480)",

            {
                "content": "Yes, there are common factors that can contribute to infertility in both males and females. Here are some shared aspects:\n\n- **Age**:\n  - Advanced age affects fertility in both genders, with declining fertility rates as individuals get older.\n\n- **Hormonal Imbalances**:\n  - Hormonal issues can impact ovulation in women and sperm production in men.\n\n- **Lifestyle Factors**:\n  - Smoking, excessive alcohol consumption, poor diet, and lack of exercise can negatively affect fertility for both sexes.\n\n- **Medical Conditions**:\n  - Conditions like diabetes, thyroid disorders, and autoimmune diseases can influence reproductive health in both men and women.\n\n- **Obesity and Underweight**:\n  - Both extreme weight conditions can disrupt hormonal balance and affect fertility.\n\n- **Stress**:\n  - Psychological stress can impact both partners, potentially leading to fertility problems.\n\nWhile there are unique causes of infertility for each gender, addressing these common factors can improve overall reproductive health for both men and women.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Fertility and Sterility, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008) \n- [Human Reproduction, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1093/humrep/deac023)",

                "content": "What is male infertility",
                "role": "user"
            },
            {
                "content": "Male infertility refers to the inability of a male to cause pregnancy in a fertile female. It accounts for about 30-40% of infertility cases. Here are some key causes and factors:\n\n- **Sperm Production Issues**:\n  - Low sperm count (oligospermia) or no sperm (azoospermia) can arise from genetic defects, hormonal imbalances, or infections.\n\n- **Sperm Function Problems**:\n  - Abnormal sperm shape (morphology) or reduced motility (movement) can hinder sperm's ability to fertilize an egg.\n\n- **Anatomical Issues**:\n  - Conditions such as varicocele (enlarged veins in the scrotum) or obstruction in the reproductive tract can block sperm passage.\n\n- **Lifestyle Factors**:\n  - Smoking, excessive alcohol use, drug abuse, obesity, and poor diet negatively impact sperm health.\n\n- **Environmental Factors**:\n  - Exposure to certain chemicals, radiation, and elevated temperatures can affect sperm production.\n\n- **Medical Conditions**:\n  - Conditions like diabetes, thyroid disorders, and sexually transmitted infections can contribute to infertility.\n\nDiagnosis typically involves semen analysis and hormone testing. Treatment options may include lifestyle changes, medications, or assisted reproductive technologies.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Human Reproduction Update, 2023](https://doi.org/10.1093/humupd/dmad023)\n- [Reproductive Biology and Endocrinology, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1186/s12958-022-00801-2)",

                "content": "IVF stands for In Vitro Fertilization, a complex assisted reproductive technology used to help individuals or couples conceive. Here are the key steps involved:\n\n- **Ovarian Stimulation**:\n  - Hormonal medications are administered to stimulate the ovaries to produce multiple eggs.\n\n- **Egg Retrieval**:\n  - Once the eggs are mature, a minor surgical procedure is performed to retrieve them from the ovaries.\n\n- **Fertilization**:\n  - The retrieved eggs are combined with sperm in a laboratory setting. This can be done via standard insemination or intracytoplasmic sperm injection (ICSI).\n\n- **Embryo Culture**:\n  - Fertilized eggs (embryos) are cultured for several days to monitor development.\n\n- **Embryo Transfer**:\n  - One or more healthy embryos are transferred into the uterus.\n\n- **Pregnancy Test**:\n  - A pregnancy test is conducted a couple of weeks after the transfer to check for success.\n\nIVF is often recommended for individuals facing infertility issues, such as blocked fallopian tubes, endometriosis, or male factor infertility. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [Reproductive Medicine and Biology, 2023](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12473) \n- [Assisted reproductive technology surveillance \u2014 United States, 2019](https://doi.org/10.15585/mmwr.mm6935a2)",

                "content": "IVF (In Vitro Fertilization) is generally considered safe, but there are potential risks and complications that individuals should be aware of:\n\n- **Ovarian Hyperstimulation Syndrome (OHSS)**:\n  - Can occur due to stimulation medications, leading to painful swelling of the ovaries.\n\n- **Multiple Pregnancies**:\n  - Higher chance of twins or multiples, which can increase risks for both mother and babies.\n\n- **Ectopic Pregnancy**:\n  - A rare occurrence where an embryo implants outside the uterus, typically in a fallopian tube.\n\n- **Surgical Risks**:\n  - Risks associated with egg retrieval procedures, including bleeding, infection, and injury to surrounding organs.\n\n- **Emotional and Psychological Impact**:\n  - The IVF process can be emotionally taxing and lead to stress or anxiety for individuals and couples.\n\nWhile these risks exist, many individuals undergo IVF successfully and safely. It\u2019s important to discuss potential risks with a healthcare provider and have a supportive plan in place.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Fertility and Sterility, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008) \n- [International Society for Mild Approaches in Assisted Reproduction (ISMAR)](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12480)",

Here are some shared aspects:\n\n- **Age**:\n  - Advanced age affects fertility in both genders, with declining fertility rates as individuals get older.\n\n- **Hormonal Imbalances**:\n  - Hormonal issues can impact ovulation in women and sperm production in men.\n\n- **Lifestyle Factors**:\n  - Smoking, excessive alcohol consumption, poor diet, and lack of exercise can negatively affect fertility for both sexes.\n\n- **Medical Conditions**:\n  - Conditions like diabetes, thyroid disorders, and autoimmune diseases can influence reproductive health in both men and women.\n\n- **Obesity and Underweight**:\n  - Both extreme weight conditions can disrupt hormonal balance and affect fertility.\n\n- **Stress**:\n  - Psychological stress can impact both partners, potentially leading to fertility problems.\n\nWhile there are unique causes of infertility for each gender, addressing these common factors can improve overall reproductive health for both men and women.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Fertility and Sterility, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008) \n- [Human Reproduction, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1093/humrep/deac023)",

            {
                "content": "Male infertility refers to the inability of a male to cause pregnancy in a fertile female. It accounts for about 30-40% of infertility cases. Here are some key causes and factors:\n\n- **Sperm Production Issues**:\n  - Low sperm count (oligospermia) or no sperm (azoospermia) can arise from genetic defects, hormonal imbalances, or infections.\n\n- **Sperm Function Problems**:\n  - Abnormal sperm shape (morphology) or reduced motility (movement) can hinder sperm's ability to fertilize an egg.\n\n- **Anatomical Issues**:\n  - Conditions such as varicocele (enlarged veins in the scrotum) or obstruction in the reproductive tract can block sperm passage.\n\n- **Lifestyle Factors**:\n  - Smoking, excessive alcohol use, drug abuse, obesity, and poor diet negatively impact sperm health.\n\n- **Environmental Factors**:\n  - Exposure to certain chemicals, radiation, and elevated temperatures can affect sperm production.\n\n- **Medical Conditions**:\n  - Conditions like diabetes, thyroid disorders, and sexually transmitted infections can contribute to infertility.\n\nDiagnosis typically involves semen analysis and hormone testing. Treatment options may include lifestyle changes, medications, or assisted reproductive technologies.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Human Reproduction Update, 2023](https://doi.org/10.1093/humupd/dmad023)\n- [Reproductive Biology and Endocrinology, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1186/s12958-022-00801-2)",

                "content": "--- Similar Node 1 ---\n### Section Details\n- **Title**: The male infertility evaluation still matters in the era of high efficacy assisted reproductive technology\n- **Text**: Infertility is defined as a couple's inability to conceive after 12 months of regular, unprotected sexual intercourse (1) .Current estimates place the global burden of infertility at 48 million couples (2) .Infertility affects roughly 15% of couples of reproductive age, with a male factor being solely responsible in 20% and contributory in an additional 30% of cases (3) .Despite how often a male factor may contribute to infertility, up to 27% of men in a heterosexual relationship seeking fertility care may not even be offered a male factor infertility evaluation (4) .Evaluation of the male often is neglected because women are the usual drivers of care-seeking for fertility treatment (5) .Additionally, public and provider perception of infertility as a primarily gynecologic problem, combined with the misperception that the use of assisted reproductive technology (ART) can circumvent a male factor problem, can unwittingly minimize the importance of the male evaluation (6, 7) .This trend is worrisome because a thorough male evaluation can uncover serious underlying medical conditions, such as genetic disorders, endocrine disruptions, mood disorders, or even malignancy (8) .\n- **DOI**: 10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008\n- **Sequence**: 2\n### Next Section\n- **Text**: The 2021 joint guidelines from the American Urological Association and the American Society for Reproductive Medicine emphasize the ongoing importance of the male factor evaluation in the context of an infertile couple (9) .Per guidelines, men with R1 abnormal semen parameters, presumed male infertility, and men in couples with failed ART cycles or recurrent pregnancy losses, should be evaluated by a male fertility specialist.In addition to detecting occult male somatic pathology, evaluation of the male partner can lead to improved outcomes, cost-effectiveness, and decreased physical and emotional burden for couples (See Fig. 1 ).\n- **DOI**: 10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008\n- **Sequence**: 3\n### Entities in Section\n- **Entity Name**: Hereditary Diseases\n  - **CUI**: C0019247\n  - **Semantic Type**: Disease or Syndrome\n  - **Definitions**:\n    - Genetic diseases are diseases in which inherited genes predispose to increased risk. The genetic disorders associated with cancer often result from an alteration or mutation in a single gene. The diseases range from rare dominant cancer family syndrome to familial tendencies in which low-penetrance genes may interact with other genes or environmental factors to induce cancer. Research may involve clinical, epidemiologic, and laboratory studies of persons, families, and populations at high risk of these disorders.\n    - general term for any disorder caused by a genetic mechanism, comprising chromosome aberrations or anomalies, mendelian or monogenic or single-gene disorders, and multifactorial disorders; note that all genetic disorders are not treed under this term; see RTs for others; in addition, many disorders not treed here or under RTs may have a genetic component.\n    - <p>Genes are the building blocks of heredity. They are passed from parent to child. They hold DNA, the instructions for making proteins. Proteins do most of the work in cells. They move molecules from one place to another, build structures, break down toxins, and do many other maintenance jobs.</p> <p>Sometimes there is a mutation, a change in a gene or genes. The mutation changes the gene's instructions for making a protein, so the protein does not work properly or is missing entirely. This can cause a medical condition called a genetic disorder.</p> <p>You can inherit a gene mutation from one or both parents. A mutation can also happen during your lifetime.</p> <p>There are three types of genetic disorders:</p> <ul> <li>Single-gene disorders, where a mutation affects one gene. Sickle cell anemia is an example.</li> <li>Chromosomal disorders, where chromosomes (or parts of chromosomes) are missing or changed. Chromosomes are the structures that hold our genes. Down syndrome is a chromosomal disorder.</li> <li>Complex disorders, where there are mutations in two or more genes. Often your lifestyle and environment also play a role. Colon cancer is an example.</li> </ul> <p><a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/genetictesting.html\">Genetic tests</a> on blood and other tissue can identify genetic disorders.</p> <p class=\"\">NIH: National Library of Medicine</p>\n    - Diseases caused by genetic mutations that are inherited from a parent's genome.\n- **Entity Name**: Disease\n  - **CUI**: C0012634\n  - **Semantic Type**: Disease or Syndrome\n  - **Definitions**:\n    - Conceptually broad term referring primarily to physical illness. Here are the key steps involved:\n\n- **Ovarian Stimulation**:\n  - Hormonal medications are administered to stimulate the ovaries to produce multiple eggs.\n\n- **Egg Retrieval**:\n  - Once the eggs are mature, a minor surgical procedure is performed to retrieve them from the ovaries.\n\n- **Fertilization**:\n  - The retrieved eggs are combined with sperm in a laboratory setting. This can be done via standard insemination or intracytoplasmic sperm injection (ICSI).\n\n- **Embryo Culture**:\n  - Fertilized eggs (embryos) are cultured for several days to monitor development.\n\n- **Embryo Transfer**:\n  - One or more healthy embryos are transferred into the uterus.\n\n- **Pregnancy Test**:\n  - A pregnancy test is conducted a couple of weeks after the transfer to check for success.\n\nIVF is often recommended for individuals facing infertility issues, such as blocked fallopian tubes, endometriosis, or male factor infertility. \n\n**Sources**:\n- [Reproductive Medicine and Biology, 2023](https://doi.org/10.1002/rmb2.12473) \n- [Assisted reproductive technology surveillance \u2014 United States, 2019](https://doi.org/10.15585/mmwr.mm6935a2)",

million couples (2) .Infertility affects roughly 15% of couples of reproductive age, with a male factor being solely responsible in 20% and contributory in an additional 30% of cases (3) .Despite how of the male often is neglected because women are the usual drivers of care-seeking for fertility treatment (5) .Additionally, public and provider perception of infertility as a primarily gynecologic problem, combined with the misperception that the use of assisted reproductive technology (ART) can circumvent a male factor problem, can unwittingly minimize the importance of the male evaluation (6, 7) .This trend is worrisome because a thorough male evaluation can uncover serious underlying medical conditions, such as genetic disorders, endocrine disruptions, mood disorders, or even malignancy (8) .\n- **DOI**: 10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008\n- **Sequence**: 2\n### Next Section\n- **Text**: The 2021 joint guidelines from the American Urological Association and the American Society for They are passed from parent to child. They hold DNA, the instructions for making proteins. Proteins do most of the work in cells. They move molecules from one place to another, build structures, break down toxins, and do many other maintenance jobs.</p> <p>Sometimes there is a mutation, a change in a gene or genes. The mutation changes the gene's instructions for making a protein, so the protein does not work properly or is missing entirely. This can cause a medical condition called a genetic disorder.</p> <p>You can inherit a gene mutation from one or both parents. A mutation can also happen during your lifetime.</p> <p>There are three types of genetic disorders:</p> <ul> <li>Single-gene disorders, where a mutation affects one gene. Sickle cell anemia is an example.</li> <li>Chromosomal disorders, where chromosomes (or parts of chromosomes) are missing or changed. Chromosomes are the structures that hold our genes. Down syndrome is a chromosomal disorder.</li> <li>Complex disorders, where there are mutations in two or more genes. Often your lifestyle and environment also play a role. Colon cancer is an example.</li> </ul> <p><a href=\"https://medlineplus.gov/genetictesting.html\">Genetic tests</a> on blood and other tissue can identify genetic disorders.</p> <p class=\"\">NIH: National Library of Medicine</p>\n    - Diseases caused by genetic mutations that are inherited from a parent's genome.\n- **Entity Name**: Disease\n  - **CUI**: C0012634\n  - **Semantic Type**: Disease or Syndrome\n  - **Definitions**:\n    - Conceptually broad term referring primarily to physical illness. This means that it is a drug that slows down brain activity. It can change your mood, behavior, and self-control. It can cause problems with memory and thinking clearly. Alcohol can also affect your coordination and physical control.</p> <p>Alcohol also has effects on the other organs in your body. For example, it can raise your blood pressure and heart rate. If you drink too much at once, it could make you throw up.</p> <h3>Why are the effects of alcohol different from person to person?</h3> <p>Alcohol's effects vary from person to person, depending on a variety of factors, including:</p><ul> <li>How much you drank</li> <li>How quickly you drank it</li> <li>The amount of food you ate before drinking</li> <li>Your age</li> <li>Your sex</li> <li>Your race or ethnicity</li> <li>Your physical condition</li> <li>Whether or not you have a family history of alcohol problems</li> </ul> <h3>What is moderate drinking?</h3> <ul> <li>For most women, moderate drinking is no more than one standard drink a day</li> <li>For most men, moderate drinking is no more than two standard drinks a day</li> </ul> <p>Even though moderate drinking may be safe for many people, there are still risks. For a man, this usually happens after having 5 or more drinks within a few hours. For a woman, it is after about 4 or more drinks within a few hours.</li> <li>Heavy alcohol use is having having more than 4 drinks on any day for men or more than 3 drinks for women</li> </ul> <p>Binge drinking raises your risk of injuries, car crashes, and alcohol poisoning. Here are the key points about ICSI:\n\n- **Procedure**:\n  - In ICSI, a single sperm is injected directly into an egg to facilitate fertilization, bypassing potential barriers in sperm function.\n\n- **Indications**:\n  - Commonly used for male infertility issues, such as low sperm count (oligospermia), poor sperm motility, or if previous IVF attempts have failed.\n\n- **Advantages**:\n  - Increases the chances of fertilization when traditional IVF methods might not be effective, especially in cases of severe male factor infertility.\n\n- **Success Rates**:\n  - Generally, similar to conventional IVF, but specific success may vary based on individual factors like age and reproductive health.\n\n- **Risks**:\n  - Includes potential for multiple pregnancies, ovarian hyperstimulation syndrome (OHSS), or rare complications associated with the egg retrieval process.\n\nICSI has revolutionized the approach to treating male infertility and is often a component of standard IVF protocols.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Human Reproduction Update, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1093/humupd/dmam051)\n- [Fertility and Sterility, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008)",

                "content": "Male infertility refers to the inability of a male to cause pregnancy in a fertile female. It accounts for about 30-40% of infertility cases. Here are some key causes and factors:\n\n- **Sperm Production Issues**:\n  - Low sperm count (oligospermia) or no sperm (azoospermia) can arise from genetic defects, hormonal imbalances, or infections.\n\n- **Sperm Function Problems**:\n  - Abnormal sperm shape (morphology) or reduced motility (movement) can hinder sperm's ability to fertilize an egg.\n\n- **Anatomical Issues**:\n  - Conditions such as varicocele (enlarged veins in the scrotum) or obstruction in the reproductive tract can block sperm passage.\n\n- **Lifestyle Factors**:\n  - Smoking, excessive alcohol use, drug abuse, obesity, and poor diet negatively impact sperm health.\n\n- **Environmental Factors**:\n  - Exposure to certain chemicals, radiation, and elevated temperatures can affect sperm production.\n\n- **Medical Conditions**:\n  - Conditions like diabetes, thyroid disorders, and sexually transmitted infections can contribute to infertility.\n\nDiagnosis typically involves semen analysis and hormone testing. Treatment options may include lifestyle changes, medications, or assisted reproductive technologies.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Human Reproduction Update, 2023](https://doi.org/10.1093/humupd/dmad023)\n- [Reproductive Biology and Endocrinology, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1186/s12958-022-00801-2)",

        "content": "ICSI, or Intracytoplasmic Sperm Injection, is a specialized form of in vitro fertilization (IVF). Here are the key points about ICSI:\n\n- **Procedure**:\n  - In ICSI, a single sperm is injected directly into an egg to facilitate fertilization, bypassing potential barriers in sperm function.\n\n- **Indications**:\n  - Commonly used for male infertility issues, such as low sperm count (oligospermia), poor sperm motility, or if previous IVF attempts have failed.\n\n- **Advantages**:\n  - Increases the chances of fertilization when traditional IVF methods might not be effective, especially in cases of severe male factor infertility.\n\n- **Success Rates**:\n  - Generally, similar to conventional IVF, but specific success may vary based on individual factors like age and reproductive health.\n\n- **Risks**:\n  - Includes potential for multiple pregnancies, ovarian hyperstimulation syndrome (OHSS), or rare complications associated with the egg retrieval process.\n\nICSI has revolutionized the approach to treating male infertility and is often a component of standard IVF protocols.\n\n**Sources**:\n- [Human Reproduction Update, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1093/humupd/dmam051)\n- [Fertility and Sterility, 2022](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.fertnstert.2022.05.008)",
